So before I begin, I'd like to say that it is pointless to expect songs like \\"Jeremy\\", \\"Black\\" or \\"Rearviewmirror\\" from a band that has grown so much in the past few years.  Pearl Jam are probably not in the same places they once were, and I hope you and I aren't either.  <br />So, as I pop in the disc, I am stunned by the passion of the vocals that I hear; the swirling ringing of guitar, bass, drums and vocals that is \\"Can't Keep.\\"  Contrary to what else I have read from a few people, Eddie sounds more passionate and emotional than ever.  You can just hear the emotion quivering from his voice.  And to the person that said \\"Thumbing My Way\\" was emotionless, did you even listen or pay attention to the lyrics?  \\"All the rusted signs we ignore throughout our lives/choosing the shiny ones instead/I turned my back/now there's no turning back.\\"  The way Eddie sings those lines make me remember how I felt the first time I heard \\"Release.\\"  Not to mention how beautiful a composition \\"Love Boat Captain\\" is, with a melody that is bleeding with intensity not felt by other bands these days.  \\"Save You\\" is a frustrated emotional explosion \\"Too important to us/we'd be lost without you/let yourself fall/i'm right below you,\\" while in \\"1/2 Full\\" Eddie spits \\"Don't see some men as 1/2 empty/see them 1/2 full of sh*t.\\"  Yeaaaah.   <br />Even more suprisingly incredible than the lyrics is how the music sounds alive in ways that you might not expect.  Matt Cameron feels more connected with the band than in Binaural, and seems to have acheived that great 'tight-looseness' that the band had with Dave Abruzzeese back on Vs and Vitalogy.  Jeff always rocks, but Mike and Stone really work magic on this album, from beginning to end, sounding off with contrastingly interesting parts at all the right moments, such as on the bluesy rock jam \\"1/2 Full,\\" and sounding like a huge wall of sound where it's incredibly appropriate, like on the brilliant rocker \\"Get Right.\\"  <br />Yes, everyone has their opinion.  I just feel that anyone who doesn't like this record must be expecting something that they have heard before, and from the bottom of my soul I feel that is the worst way to approach new music.  Just because Pearl Jam invented a distinct sound way back in the early 90's doesn't mean they have to cling to it.  They are showing their versatility and talent in performing different songs such as \\"You Are\\" and \\"Green Disease\\" with such effectiveness as well as courage.  I could have done without \\"Bushleaguer,\\" but hey, self expression is self expression.  The sound of the record is something else to consider, as the music sounds clearer and crisper than recent pearl jam albums.  Riot Act sounds like it has the clarity of Vs. with the good bottom end sound of Yield.  The production and mixing is quite well done, and going back and listening to Binaural makes that album sound like a muddy mess at times.  Kudos to Adam Kasper and Brendan O' brien for making a great sounding band sound even better.  <br />Coming from a music fan that loves equally Tool, Opeth, Peter Gabriel and Wes Montgomery, do yourself a favor and pick this up.  Anyone who doesn't recommend this did not give this album the chance it deserved.  There's something for EVERY Pearl Jam fan here.  $12-16 is a small price to pay for quality music like this.

B000007MVX|5.0|_A Passion Play_ is probably Jethro Tull's most dark, dense,  and unfathomable album. It followed the same lengthy one-track formula as the previous album _Thick As A Brick_. The concept on here seems to center around a man who was just deceased. He wonders whether he wants to go to heaven, hell or to be reborn--that's my take on it. While the combination of dense lyrics, wry humor and biblical imagery may hinder the &quot;full&quot; understanding of the concept, the complex musicianship found on this 45-minute work is top-notch. Ian Anderson and the boys seem to kick up their compositional/writing insights a notch more than they did on previous albums. On here, you'll find organic forms of jazz, classical and folk blended nicely with sophisticated rock. The technical prowess displayed by all the musicians on here is awe-inspiring and masterful. Ian Anderson takes on the flute, two kinds of saxaphones and acoustic guitars. He handles them all quite well.<br />_A Passion Play_ is a work that may require many listens before being appreciated to it's fullest. It's a work that features moments that can bring fright, melancholy and sinister laughs to many. It's a work that many people can come to appreciate after they've soaked up Jethro Tull's (or anyone else's) most accessible work, then realize it's true beauty, genius and transcendence.

B00005YXZH|5.0|It's not hard to figure out why this CD's average rating is one star lower than it should be.  Yankee Hotel Foxtrot has received media scrutiny from all outlets, and particularly because of the label snafu and the Sam Means film.  When there's no pressure on a record to perform, a listener who's pleasantly surprised might get overzealous and give five stars.  However, there's a whole set of built-in expectations whenever a record receives top ratings in the New York Times, Rolling Stone Magazine, Alternative Press, CMJ, &amp;c.  People feel cheated if their initial experience with an album of this sort is anything short of orgasmic.<br />There are good reasons, however, to love this album.  Most obviously, the rest of Wilco's catalogue (which included some pretty good stuff) is entirely unlistenable after you hear this album.  The extra attention to texture, pitch, timbre, dynamics, rhythms, &amp;c., might lead to some slip-ups, but the overall sound of this album is far richer and more complex than anything the band, and probably the sub-genre, has ever produced.  And simply put, the songwriting and instrumentation are often beautiful.<br />Furthermore, the album traces several arcs.  Most apparent is the dynamic buildup and layering of songs, and effects from start to finish.  Equally important is the fact that the album is seen as one cohesive unit, rather than a diverse collection of singles.  Themes from the first track are picked up later on, and rhythms and songwriting devices are recycled.  Despite this, each track manages a unique freshness.<br />You may think this album's a little pretentious, or that Tweedy's voice isn't exactly a thing of beauty.  You'd be right on both counts.  But Wilco is so far ahead of the FM radio crowd, and so far ahead of the more &quot;indie scene&quot; that this album is a necessity, no matter what your musical tastes.

This one has built a following over the years for good reason; anyone interested in filmmaking and technique should watch this film.

0449227421|2.0|On a recent trip to Florida I stopped in at the airport book shop and purchased a book for the return flight to Italy. I had read Ken Follet's excellent &quot;Pillars of the Earth&quot; a few years ago and decided to try another of his books. This book did not even approach the level of the first Follet novel I read. Unlike &quot;Pillars&quot;, in &quot;The Third Twin&quot; Follet does not appear to have done any research on his subject matter. In one section he has a character reminiscing about his father who was a SECOND LIEUTENANT in the US NAVY. The US Navy has never had second lieutenants; junior officers of that pay grade are called Ensigns. But this is only the beginning of technical errors that destroy an otherwise compelling story line. One of the main weapons of the protagonist is a computer program that she wrote to compare elements in database to develop genetic comparisons. Unfortunately, the way the thing is supposed to work is closer to something that Gandalf or Harry Potter would whip up rather than something out of computer science class. The poorly researched and unbelievable stuff goes on and worsens throughout the book. More examples - people out on bail without a security clearance wandering the halls of the Pentagon and putting magic floppy disks into the Pentagon computers - you guessed it - so they could search the databases on those computers. All in all, I was very, very glad when the book drew to its predictable close. <br />Follet is a good writer, but he really needs to research his subjects better before writing a book.
B00005B36H|5.0|It's about time I reviewed this. It's been one of my favorite albums since the day it was released, and was the first cd that I really anticipated the release of. No cd have I listened to more intensely during the weeks just after it's purchase than this one. It's almost beyond reviewing. It's hard to really say why it's so good, beyond just to say it rules. Hopefully, I can elaborate on precisely why it rules.<br />People like to criticize Tool for not being true Virtuoso musicians.(Conversely people criticize Dream Theater for focusing too much on their technical skills. Both groups are morons.) I don't see how anyone can have a problem with the rhythm section. Carey is a great drummer, easily the most distinctive I have heard, particularly on this album. No one plays patterns like him, and he can still lay down the hyper-speed barrage of thrash and death when he wants to.(listen to Ticks&Leeches) Chancellor is one of the best metal bassists out there, primarily because he is simply a much more significant piece of the band than most are.  His hypnotic, flowing bass-lines provide the starting point for the albums winding structures, rather than him simply adding power to the guitar parts.(though he still does that a lot) He and Adam Jones have easily the best interplay of any bassist and guitarist I have ever heard.(Come to think of it, Carey and Chancellor have the best interplay of any rhythm section I've ever heard.) People try and tell me Adam Jones is a bad guitar player, and I don't really buy it. I don't play the guitar, and while his stuff doesn't sound that hard, I frankly have no real idea, and I doubt most of the reviewers who criticize him really know what the hell they are talking about. He's no John Petrucci or Dave Mustaine, but so what. He's doesn't try to be like them. He's about mood and texture and is frankly much more creative than either of those guitarists I mentioned.  Maynard holds it all together. He's long been my favorite vocalist, and I don't forsee my opinion changing any time soon.  He can snarl with real rage and drone robotically too, and everything in between. Once again, he doesn't have a stunning range or technique, but has an other world intensity to him which is simply unmatched. He's probably not as good here as he is on there previous 2 albums, but he still crushes most anyone else.<br />Some people have tried to complain that this album doesn't have much music, that it \\"only has 3-4 real songs\\". These people need to learn to count. This album has 6 (somewhat) conventional songs, and one ultra-long composition that isn't really properly described as a song, but is made up entirely of \\"actual music\\". All in all, this album has over 70 minutes of \\"actual music\\" which is a whole lot more than most cds you'll buy. There isn't a bad song on this album. The Grudge is the opener. It's a solid rocker, with some intense drum work and a great flow to the song as a whole, with the basic riff slowly mutating throughout it. The guitars are great, with gentle and hypnotic portions giving way to some truly intense pounding. Maynard's vocals really shine here too. He uses a monotonous drone as the bass style, which gives way to the envenomed shouting and a slower, gentler style as well. The next two tracks Eon Von Blue Apocalypse/The Patient make up the next full song. Eon Von... is just an intro with gentle, eastern guitars, which continue into the beginning of The Patient, which builds to the lumbering metal assault that characterizes the second half of the song, which also includes some nice multi-part vocals. Schism is the next song, and the first single to the album. It is arguably the weakest songs, but is still very cool. It's sort of a toned down The Grudge, with a similar vocal style to it, and a similar basic structure, with main riff slowly being built upon and then dismantled again. Parabol/Parabola is another single, though they sadly cut leave off Parabol, even though it is clearly part of the same song. Parabol and Parabola are the soft/loud, gentle/violent counterparts to each other, with the Parabola part being one of the heavier, more unrelentinly intense parts of this album, particularly the slow, brooding instrumental section that closes the song. Tick&Leeches is very cool. It's a return to the more hostile, enraged Tool of yore. This is one of the heavier songs they've done. Carey's drums simply astound, and Maynard gives one of his most vitriolic vocal performances to date. The quiet bridge in the middle is even pretty cool, though I feel it should be a little shorter. Lateralus is probably the single best song on the album. It was also a single, though the radio jerks usually cut out the middle 2 1/2 minutes, which really hurts it. The introduction is just beautiful, and it creates a good sense anticipation for the song itself. It's got more inhuman drumming, particularly during the verses, and a good mix of power-chord pounding and more atmospheric squealing from Jones. The middle section is the key to the song, with more stunning vocal work. The epic composition of Disposition/Reflection/Triad follows, which is easily the most ambitious thing Tool has ever done to this point in their career. Reflection, at 11 minutes is the centerpiece of the composition, and is a stunning piece at that. It's flowing and softer than most Tool work, with a rather psychadelic feel to much of it. I would try to describe it further, but to do so is essentially pointless. Just listen to it yourself. Triad is an extremely unusual, and therefore very appropriate closing section. It's an instrumental section, with an odd, half industrial, half tribal beat to it, an excellent use of some very strange sound effects and some of Jones best near avant-garde guitar squealing combined with some pounding and stunningly thick sounding rhythm work. Faaip de Oiad is the other not really music track. Still, the drum work in it is very cool.<br />Tool rules. Buy Tool now.f the same song. B00006JL1Z|5.0|I don't normally review &quot;Best of&quot;s and live albums - too much of a &quot;been-there-done-that&quot; thing going on - but, like the Tom Petty anthology of a few years ago, there's a completeness and comprehensiveness to THE VERY BEST OF FLEETWOOD MAC that can't be ignored (the fact that Fleetwood Mac is my favorite band of all time probably has something to do with it as well!). The disc could very easily be subtitled &quot;The Singles&quot;, as it includes almost every song released by the band as a single between 1975 and 1992. But that's only part of the set's charm....there is still a vitality and energy to this music that's enchanting, even now. I dare anyone to listen to these songs and not have one or two jump out at them, reminding them of some one, some time, some place or some thing. Highlights for me include &quot;Monday Morning&quot;, the opening track on Disc One, with it's energetic Buckingham vocal and in-your-face guitar, and &quot;Dreams&quot;, the band's only (believe it or not!) #1 song. With it's bass line pounding like a heartbeat and Nicks cooing her poetry-like verses, this song is as powerful as it was twenty-five years ago! Then there's &quot;Go Your Own Way&quot;, a song that's appeared on practically every &quot;Best Of&quot; list over the last decade. This is angry rock &amp; roll, both literally and figuratively, and it's biting attack jumps out of the speakers at you still. Then there's &quot;Silver Springs.&quot; I'm so glad this track has finally gotten it's just dues in the last five years; with it's tinkling keyboards, soaring backing vocals and emotion-drenched performance by Nicks, this is a true classic that deserved better than the long-out-of-print, non-album B-side fate it had suffered for twenty years. &quot;Love In Store&quot; is a melodic rocker that presents Fleetwood Mac at it's best....tight playing, a creamy lead vocal by Christine McVie and that unparallelled harmony vocalizing from Buckingham and Nicks. Why this wasn't a bigger hit upon it's release in '82 still amazes me! &quot;Tusk&quot; is still as fun as ever, while the beauty and simplicity of &quot;Songbird&quot; never grows old. Closing out the disc is &quot;Storms&quot;, an album track of Nicks' that still gives me chills. &quot;Haunting&quot; is the only way to describe this beautifully-sung, brilliantly-arranged masterpiece. Disc Two, though, has to be my favorite. You've got &quot;Second Hand News&quot;, with it's slightly naughty (for 1977!) overtones, and &quot;Little Lies&quot;, probably one of the two or three best singles ever recorded by the band. Then there's &quot;Think About Me&quot;, a churning rocker that was sadly overlooked in 1980. I still find &quot;Hold Me&quot; as magical as I did in 1982, while &quot;Seven Wonders&quot; is still the &quot;classic-waiting-to-happen&quot; that I thought it was in '87. &quot;World Turning&quot; still has that &quot;in-your-face&quot;/jamming-in-the-studio vibe to it, probably the main song off of FLEETWOOD MAC that hinted at all the good things yet to come, while &quot;Sisters Of The Moon&quot; is the &quot;Little single that never got a chance.&quot; Released as the last single off of TUSK, it quickly disappeared. Don't let that fool you....with it's sinister guitar and forboding lyrics about clawing fans and wasting away to the point of being unrecognizable, this song is as eerie and mesmerizing as it was in 1979. &quot;Family Man&quot; is goofy fun, while &quot;As Long As You Follow&quot; still floats like a butterfly and stings like.....a stolen kiss. I'm also glad to see they chose &quot;Skies The Limit&quot; to represent BEHIND THE MASK versus the much weaker &quot;Save Me.&quot; Wrapping it all up is the springboard beat of &quot;Paper Doll&quot;, another strong song from Nicks that deserved a better fate than the one it encountered in 1992. Soooo.....as much as I love this set, is there anything I would have done differently? Oh, yeah! Besides cutting back on one or two of the album tracks from RUMOURS, I would have used this chance to finally release &quot;Ricky&quot; on an album. This non-album B-side from the &quot;Little Lies&quot; single is amazing, unlike any other vocal by Christine McVie we've ever seen. I would have also swapped out one of Buckingham's tracks from RUMOURS with &quot;Oh Diane&quot; from MIRAGE, as well as included the studio version of &quot;Big Love.&quot; As for Miss Nicks, I would gladly have traded &quot;No Questions Asked&quot; - the weakest song she's EVER recorded!- with &quot;Angel&quot; from TUSK, &quot;That's Alright&quot; from MIRAGE or &quot;Love Is Dangerous&quot; from BEHIND THE MASK. Which brings us to my only other bone of contention - there is no mention whatsoever of Billy Burnette or Rick Vito anywhere in the liner notes, even though they played on four of the songs (Vito's writing credit is even removed from &quot;Paper Doll!&quot;). I know this set was meant to showcase &quot;The Big Five&quot;, but it's just not fair to act as it these two weren't part of the band during this period. Vito and Burnette deserved better. That said, pick up THE VERY BEST OF FLEETWOOD MAC and get reacquainted with some old friends. I guarantee that you will be transported back to - to quote Stevie Nicks on &quot;Seven Wonders&quot; - &quot;a certain time,...a certain place!&quot;ack on one or two of the album tracks from RUMOURS, I would have used this chance to finally release &quot;Ricky&quot; on an album. B00006J9OT|5.0|Diana Krall has been hailed my many as one of the great jazz musicians of the modern era. After hearing her last album, the wildly successful The Look of Love, I couldn't understand why. Sure, it was recorded well, but to these ears, it was little more than a cure for insomnia. It seemed like her management was more interested with marketing her based on her looks, rather than her musical abilities. But now, on her newest release, Live in Paris, I can hear what all the fuss has been about. <br />Accompanied by her core band--Anthony Wilson on guitar, John Clayton on bass, and Jeff Hamilton on drums--Krall wows the crowd time and time again. Then again, it's hard to go wrong when most of the material is coming from the Great American Songbooks of Cole Porter, Harold Arlen, George and Ira Gershwin. <br />Recorded live over four nights at the historic Olympia Theatre, Live in Paris gives you a firsthand account of the ambience and excitement of a musical evening with her. The only thing missing is the stage banter you'd hear at a `real' show. Krall is actually very talkative during her shows and (as anyone who's had the pleasure of seeing here perform will tell you) the fact that the interaction with her audience is missing is a disappointment. <br />The majority of the songs are performed by her core band. They're joined by the Orchestre Symphonique Europeen on \\"Let's Fall in Love\\" and \\"I've Got You Under My Skin.\\" In addition, percussionist Paulinho Da Costa joins them on 4 tracks, and John Pisano contributes acoustic guitar on 3 tracks. However, make no mistakes. As great as her accompanying musicians may be, this is Krall's show. Swinging like there's no tomorrow, tracks like \\"I Love Being Here With You,\\" \\"'Deed I Do\\" and \\"Devil May Care\\" are the disc's biggest highlights. <br />She doesn't slack off on the ballads either, though. Her distinctive phrasing and cool, sultry vocals turn the songs into true works of art. Her stunning cover of Joni Mitchell's \\"A Case ofYou\\" is a perfect example. The only drawback, if you can call it one, is the extra studio track here--a cover of the wedding reception staple, Billy Joel's \\"Just the Way You Are.\\" It's not that it's bad (it's a straight rendition of the song); it's more a case of just being unnecessary. <br />All told, Live in Paris is an excellent place to start for those who may not be familiar with the Grammy Award-winning musician, and longtime fans are sure to enjoy it as well. The only bad thing about this CD is that it's too short.

B000007T1M|4.0|A lot of people who know about System will show off how 'old school' they are and brag about \\"how Toxicity just ...compared to the first album.\\"  Many of these folks are hard-pressed to give you a specific reason behind this statement, however.  What exactly, if anything, makes their first album better?  What are the differences/similarities between the two?  Which one is the better buy?  Where are we going and why am I in this handbasket?  I'll try and help you solve these and other questions, if only you continue reading the most helpful review I could produce for you kids. <br />The reasons that I, personally, prefer System's self-titled album to Toxicity are numerous and simple.  Here's a quick break-down.<br />1.)  The first album is heavier, more aggressive, more 'metal'. <BR>2.)  The first album is more creative and fresh than Toxicity.<BR>3.)  The first album seems to represent the band better as a whole.<BR>4.)  The songwriting on self-titled seems more experimental, over-the-top.  The same is often argued in favor of Toxicity instead; I disagree.<BR>5.)  Serj's vocals and lyrics are funnier, more political on self-titled.<BR>6.)  If hear some Linkin Park fan talk about how great \\"Chop Suey!\\" is one more time, I'm going to pass a stone...why is this STILL happening?<br />In short, I think that System's is much more intense, heavy, and brilliant than Toxicity, which, while still an excellent release, is rather drab and toned-down when compared to the original material.  All kinds of critics and writers are hailing Toxicity as one of the greatest metal albums ever...give me a break.  While not all of the songs on this album are truly outstanding, they all have their great moments, whether it be some riff or drum timing change, or some crazy vocal peice or whatever, and I think it's safe to say that the songs are more memorable than the second album.  Let's take a look at some of the more stand-out songs on their own merit, and cut with the comparissons for amoment.<br />Song 2, \\"Know\\".  A social commentative song from Serj, which is the kind of song he writes best, I think.  Great drum section from John, too, really kicks up the album's energy up a notch.  Lots of screaming with aggressive bass and guitar riffs to match.  <br />Song 3, \\"Sugar\\".  Chances are, if you've heard any song on this album before, it's this one or \\"Spiders\\".  Admittedly, they're the singles that turned me onto the band in the first place, and I still love both songs.  This one is basically just a fun, fast-paced tune to get your blood pumping and your body movin'.  Addictive vocal hook that will have you singing along instantly, even if you can't figure out what the hell he's saying.<br />Song 4, \\"Suggestions\\".  If you combined Primus, Jane's Addiction, and Metallica, you might get a song like this.  Shavo's rhythmic, pounding bassline compliments the schizophrenic guitar part, perfectly.  Contains an undeniable 'laugh out loud' moment at the beginning of the third verse.  Genius.  The predecessor to that \\"Chop Suey!\\" #1 single thing...<br />Song 5, \\"Spiders\\".  Total change of pace for the album, even the band itself, but it works.  Very dark, moody, haunting...great lyrics with an absolutely chilling guitar riff will have you putting this one on repeat for awhile.  Most emotional, serious song on the album, without a doubt.  One of the scariest basslines ever, I think it's safe to say.<br />Song 6, \\"DDevil\\".  It'd just like to point out the fabulous drum/bass breakdown 12 seconds into the song.  That is all.<br />Song 8, \\"War?\\".  Brilliance!  This is my absolute favorite System song, to this day.  \\"War?\\" is a perfect example of what system is all about, I think...part thrash, part alt-metal, even a trace of hardcore punk thrown in, with great political flavor and a quirky sound overall.  This song defines System of a Down.  You'll have the chorus (\\"Of war, we don't speak any more of war...\\") stuck in your head in no time.  WE WILL FIGHT THE HEATHANS!<br />Song 13, \\"P.L.U.C.K.\\"  Probably the most blatant political song on the album, but doesn't take itself too seriously (or lightly, for that matter).  Great way to close off one of the most outstanding metal albums of the era.<br />In conclusion, I strongly recommend this album to anyone who hasn't heard it before, or who simply hasn't had a chance to pick it up yet.  If you're worried that it's a little TOO different from Toxicity, don't worry, it isn't like they totally remodled their sound or whathaveyou...but I think that the musicianship and songwriting on this one is far superior anything they've done since, so check it out.  Amazon's got a great price on it, too...as I write this, it's just over [$].  Can't beat that.  I've heard this band described as 'Rage Against the Machine meets TooL' more than once, and even though those two just happen to be my favorite bands of all time, I disagree.  They have some elements from either band, but they're more original than that.  With this album, System invented a sound and feel purely their own, and I hope they're able to stick with it, as well as with each other, for a long time coming.<br />Ditch that \\"Aeriels\\" junk and pick up this one, baby.  You'll be glad you did.

B0000046ZJ|5.0|This CD presents a very important collaboration. Stan Getz may be the most lyrical tenor saxophone player to have ever graced the face of the earth. Only Lester Young comes close. Prez modeled his sound on that of Frankie Trumbauer's C-melody saxophone, and Getz' model is Prez. Both of them transcend Trumbauer's syrupy improvisations. Getz' tone and the lyricism it supports are evident. All of this is for context, of course. This recording is truly a major musical event. It was on a par with Lester Young's famous collaborations with Teddy Wilson and Oscar Peterson. During this 1957 recording session, all of which is on the CD, Peterson's trio and Getz were more than comfortable with one another, and their mutual musical respect yielded classic performances . The cooperation is evident in Getz' solo on \\"I Want to Be Happy,\\" a model of precision and lyrical invention. Peterson's solos are equally impressive; there's no unnecessary embellishment or decoration, and he swings powerfully. The long ballad medley--\\"Bewitched, Bothered, and Bewildered,\\" \\"I Don't Know Why, I Just Do,\\" \\"How Long Has This Been Going On?\\", \\"I Can't Get Started,\\" and \\"Polka Dots and Moonbeams\\"--features fine solos by Getz and Peterson as well as Herb Ellis on guitar and Ray Brown on bass. In the interplay between the trio and Getz in such numbers as Getz' \\"Bronx Blues.\\" the quartet sounds like a permanent group. In \\"Three Little Words\\" Ellis sets down a solid rhythm and Peterson comps behind Getz' opening solo before delivering an eloquent statement of his own which Getz answers with equal grace. The Getz-Peterson collaboration is particularly appealing on Ellis' \\"Detour Ahead,\\" a prime instance of their treatment of ballad material. On \\"Sunday\\" Ray Brown's persistent bass and Ellis' percussive guitar support Getz admirably. The quartet even approximates a rhythm 'n blues song with Getz' \\"Blues for Herky.\\" It's not every day that you hear Herb Ellis playing blues guitar with Oscar Peterson doingboogie woogie and Stan Getz blowing like he was in a jump band. But here it is, and it comes as a surprise bonus with some really superb jazz.

B000002ADT|5.0|I've must have listened to this recording many times over the last thirty years and I've never gotten bored with it.  This recording can be enjoyed by casual jazz fans and jazz fanatics and musician types like myself.<br />The music is very &quot;accessible&quot; as opposed to the heavy hard bop that turn non-jazzers off.  Yet, for all its accesibility, the music swings from the first beat of the crash cymbal that starts &quot;So What&quot; to the cool &quot;All Blues&quot;.  The haiku-like &quot;Blue in Green&quot; is the closest to a Jazz tone poem I've ever heard before or since.  If i were to characterize this recording with one word it would be &quot;understatement&quot;(maybe that's two words).<br />The personnel: Miles Davis,trumpet;John Coltrane,ten. sax;Cannonball Adderly,alto sax;Paul Chambers, bass; Wynton Kelly and Bill Evans on piano; J. Cobb on drums, have all been freed from the strictures of chord fragments and progressions to create and develop any improvistional idea they could dream up.  This was some revolutionary stuff in the pop song dominated hard bop repertoire of that era. With the exception of &quot;Blue in Green&quot; all the tunes are based on modal scales. Which freed the musicians to play anything they wanted within that scale.<br />Coltrane is superb. It's one of his last dates with Miles before recording exclusively as a leader.  His ideas are muted, inventive and never tired.  Cannonball complemented the horn section with bright,exuberant and rapid fire phrasing.  Miles' &quot;less is more&quot; approach to playing on all of the tunes tie the whole album together into a seamless masterpiece, yes masterpiece.  The spaces between his notes are as much of the music as the notes played.   <br />If you are a musician and want to play with the CD, I would suggest you purchase versions that correct for the original pressing being recorded at a slightly faster speed than the music was actually performed. This resulted in the tones being a half step higher resulting in slightly sharper tones.  If your ear won't or can't detect that, it really doesn't matter which version you pick up just as long as you add this to your collection.  You won't be disappointed.

B000001FDE|5.0|In 1969 session guitarist Duane Allman put together what would become one of the best rock bands in America. The lineup included two drummers(Jaimo Johanson & Butch Trucks), bass player (Berry Oakley), singer and organist (brother Greg Allman) and another lead guitarist (Richard Betts). What was immediately notable, apart from two drummers, was the way Duane Allman and Richard Betts played twin lead guitars - either in harmony or taking turns at solos. The music on the first four albums ('The Allman Brothers Band', 'Idlewild South', 'At Filmore East' & 'Eat a Peach') was blues rock with some jazz & country influences. What gained the band a name was their live shows with extended jams which featured the incredible guitar work by Allman & Betts. Many blues rock fans regard these albums as their finest work.<BR>In late October 1971 tragedy struck when Duane Allman was killed in a motorcycle accident. The band had been working on the fourth album 'Eat a Peach' and they now completed it with Richard Betts playing all lead & slide guitar parts. The album consisted of studio material (including the last guitar work by Duane Allman) & a live track 'Mountain Jam'. One song on the album written and sung by Richard Betts was the first indication of the sound he would bring to the band on their next album 'Brothers and Sisters'. The song was 'Blue Sky'.<BR>Instead of trying to replace the guitar of Duane Allman, the band introduced Chuck Leavell on piano as the second soloist, but while they were working on their next album, Berry Oakley was also killed in a motorcycle accident. <BR>Lamar Williams was recruited on bass and with Richard Betts as the defacto band leader the new lineup eventually finished work on the record. The completion of 'Brothers and Sisters' was a triumph over adversity and the band were rewarded with no.1 on the album charts after it's release in 1973.<BR>'Brothers and Sisters' showcased Richard Betts' ability as a great singer songwriter as well as a brilliant guitarist. Another feature was Chuck Leavell's outstanding piano work. Greg Allman contributed three tracks: 'Wasted Words'-  and  the highlights,'Come and Go Blues' & 'Jelly Jelly', - but it was Betts who  changed the focus of the band's sound with a more melodic country rock influence on the standout tracks: 'Ramblin' Man', 'Southbound', 'Jessica', and 'Pony Boy'.  <BR>'Brothers and Sisters' stands as a classic album in the history of the Allman Brothers Band and American Rock Music.
B00005R8FI|5.0|This remarkable album featured something new in blues-rock music. Mayall formed a band with no drums and no electric lead guitar, relying on the bass of Steven Thompson to keep the rhythm, and substituting acoustic \\"finger-style\\" guitar in place of the blistering electric licks that had been a prominent feature of Mayall's style. The format was not new to Mayall; the liner notes to the \\"Room to Move\\" compilation state that he got the idea from another, less well-known band. Regardless, Mayall's sound on Turning Point was unique; the use of woodwinds and acoustic guitar, combined with Mayall's harmonica, flirted around the outermost fringes of jazz, and the live tracks presented here are smooth and flowing, reaching a peak in the magnificent \\"California\\" which became a staple of FM radio for a time. \\"Room to Move\\" was the closest Mayall would ever come to a bona fide hit in the U.S.<br />Unfortunately, this was a turning point for John Mayall, commercially and artistically. Shortly after this release, blues went out of fashion, and the quality of Mayall's work plummeted. The no-drums format became increasingly difficult to work with in large, noisy concert halls and was eventually scrapped.<br />Mayall has returned to produce fine work in the 1980s and 1990s; his most recent release, \\"Stories\\", is one of his best; and he released first-rate albums such as \\"Chicago Line\\" and \\"A sense of Place\\" in the 80s. At close to 70, he is still recording and performing new work, but \\"The Turning Point\\" remains the high watermark of his career. This is one of the great albums of the 1960s, and one not to be missed.<br />This version has three \\"extra\\" tracks, and I'm happy to say that they're excellent and well worth the price of the disc, even if you own a copy of the original CD.  I wish there was more of this stuff!

B00005LLK8|5.0|Set during Northern England's miners' strike of 1984, Billy Elliot is a heart-warming, inspiring story about an eleven-year-old boy from a poor working-class family who aspires, despite the odds, to be a ballet dancer.  Billy, however, lives with his elder brother and his father, both of whom are striking miners and neither of whom can abide let alone comprehend Billy's desire to take ballet lessons.  It simply isn't done.  It's not for a lad, and that's that. <br />Billy struggles to follow both his heart and his innate talent in order to break free from a less-than-ideal (and at times even violent) home life and the prospects of a bleak future.   As a result, walls, cages, barriers and other images of entrapment figure prominently in the film, underscoring his predicament, and his dancing (by which means he struggles to break free) is powerfully juxtaposed with scenes of the violence, rage, and hatred in the community at the time.  <br />The Special Edition of the video contains a 20 minute blurb (after the closing credits) with the show's stars, producers, and director talking about the film, the characters, and the situation in England at the time, all of which is interspersed with brief clips from the film.<br />The film is a 2000 BBC co-production with a running time of 111 minutes.  Apart from a superb storyline that is powerfully portrayed, the acting is absolutely first rate by all involved.  The Northumberland accents may be a little difficult at first for some, but it's nothing that can't be overcome with close attention, and it's well worth the effort.  <br />In short, I'd highly recommend this film to anyone looking for a moving and inspiring yet rough-around-the-edges story of a lad intent upon breaking free and following his dreams in spite of the odds.  This is not some slick but gooey, maudlin Hollywood-style production, and in my opinion it's all the better for it.  The production is extremely well done--all the more so for being done in a manner so sensitive to the subject matter and the setting.

B000002HPV|4.0|Iceland is a place of wilds, and wilder wilds, where glaciers and volcanoes coexist uneasily with one another -- and the people there reflect that unusual mixture of fire and ice. Reserved on one hand, violently passionate on the other, they are probably one of the most unique, inscrutable populations on the face of the earth. <br />A perfect example is Bjork Gudmundsottir, former lead singer of the Sugarcubes, currently one of the most unique and thrilling artists in modern music. Her work is a law unto itself; like Bjork it's a distillation of her culture: rigidly arranged and produced, yet also wild and free flowing. \\"I thought I could organize freedom/how Scandinavian of me!\\" Bjork sings during \\"Hunter,\\" the first track on 1997's Homogenic -- and while the line raises a knowing smile, it's also telling that she would use such a sentiment to open the album. Homogenic was Bjork's freest, most experimental, and most challenging album up to that point. It is also her least organized album -- not necessarily a bad thing, though it does lead to missteps as the album progresses.<br />Homogenic starts promisingly, with two songs as daring as any Bjork has ever recorded. First is the aforementioned \\"Hunter,\\" with its eerie swirls of accordion and backing vocals, its dramatic, sweeping, even percussive string arrangements, and Bjork's calm, even quiet singing, in counterpoint to the restless lyrics: \\"I'm not stopping/I'm going hunting.\\" The song peaks with the climbing notes in the bridge, and Bjork's multi-tracked voice, singing the same lines again and again, until the resulting miasma of sound raises goosebumps. <br />The next song, \\"Joga,\\" is one of the singer's finest moments, describing love as a \\"state of emergency,\\" a puzzle to be solved, a \\"coincidence [that] makes sense/only with you,\\" and does so while surging over, under, and around a chorus of strings, a pulsing bassline, and background percussion that could best be described as seismic in its intensity.<BR><BR>And \\"seismic\\" is a fairly accurate description of the entire album. Whereas on previous releases the percussion, whether programmed or \\"organic,\\" was crisp and clean, here the beats sound submerged, subterranean. They rumble like ocean waves, like the erupting volcanoes and calving glaciers of Bjork's homeland. This comes up repeatedly, in songs as gentle as the romantic \\"Unravel,\\" as strident as the thrumming power-ballad \\"Bachelorette,\\" and as harsh as the dark and dissonant \\"Pluto.\\" It's a long way to this from songs like \\"Crying,\\" \\"Big Time Sensuality,\\" and \\"I Miss You\\" on previous albums; more timid listeners can hardly be blamed for not following Bjork into these newer realms of sound&amp;#61628;particularly songs such as \\"All Neon Like,\\" with its underwater- bass sound and trip-hop-dance-hall-reggae sensibility. <BR> <BR>Bjork herself seems as if she couldn't care less. She says as much in \\"5 Years,\\" where she complains about \\"cowards/that say they want/and then they can't handle,\\" and dares you to take her on, over music that is almost all distorted, compressed, programmed beats. It is the least musical song on the album; in spite of the bravery and bravado of the lyrics, it doesn't really belong here. In my opinion it shocks you out of the mood that has been so powerfully building up to that point, and I think Bjork may have gotten a little carried away on this track. But the chiming, charming \\"Immature\\" brings things back into focus, with its simple lyrics and orchestration. Bjork's vocals are in fine form, neither overpowering the song nor letting it get away from her, as on \\"5 Years.\\"<BR> <BR>\\"Alarm Call\\" sends things spinning in another direction altogether, and surprises the listener again. It's one of the most finely crafted pieces of radio fodder I've ever heard, with its poppy, bouncy bassline, its memorably singable melody, and clever \\"beep-beep\\" backing vocals. That's not the surprising part -- Bjork has proven more than once what an excellentsongwriter she is. What amazes me is that this incredible song was, with typical Bjorkian nerve, never released as a single! That speaks of supreme self-confidence, and one can't help being impressed by it. <BR> <BR>Equally impressive are the two closing tracks, \\"Pluto\\" and \\"All Is Full of Love.\\" The former song is a pounding distortion-fest that hearkens back to some of the grittier tracks on Post, such as \\"Army of Me\\" and \\"Enjoy,\\" but outstrips them both. There's a punky energy here that's missing from those songs, and Bjork really lets rip with those amazing pipes of hers, growling, howling, and roaring her way right through to the climax.<BR> <BR>\\"All Is Full of Love\\" is another of the experimental songs Bjork uses to close her albums, and this one's a wowzer. It's at once earthy and ethereal, strange and familiar; known instruments like harps, accordions and synthesizers collide with one another, becoming new. Laid over this is the singer's voice, cajoling, comforting, even chiding, rising over the enveloping sounds as she sings over and over that \\"all is full of love/you just ain't receiving\\" -- and somewhere in that voice is the promise that, if you are patient, you will receive. <BR> <BR>If one thing sums up the diverse sprawl of Homogenic, it's this: towards the end of \\"Joga,\\" the music swells up into a sweet coda of strings and rising vocal notes; then the original string arrangement comes back in, and Bjork begins singing the \\"state of emergency\\" chorus again. As she does there's a little laugh in her voice, a laugh of delight at what she's created. That's Bjork in a nutshell: taking pure delight in a workplace that is also a playground, a playground of fire and ice, where seismic sounds paint emotional landscapes. Homogenic captures that feeling as well as any of her albums, flaws and all, and in my opinion it's more than worth owning.

B00006LSM3|5.0|It's been about 15 years since George Harrison has put out a solo album, and nothing could be more eagerly anticipated than this posthumous release - and rightfully so. The 12 Brainwashed tracks that represent the work Harrison was doing over the last few years of his life are melodic, beautiful and fun. \\"Any Road\\" starts the album off on a Traveling Wilburys note - the song is bright and bouncy, and Harrison sounds as healthy as ever. The standout tracks come a little later; \\"Looking for My Life\\", \\"Rising Sun\\" and \\"Stuck Inside a Cloud\\" are absolute masterpieces, with the latter two sounding as achingly beautiful as anything he did with the Beatles or on All Things Must Pass. The guitar playing stands as some of Harrison's best and most expressive work ... just watch out for \\"Marwa Blues\\" because the song will get you right in the throat. What I was struck by most as I listened to the lyrics is the wisdom this man was blessed with from the very beginning. The things he sung about hypothetically as a young, ex-Beatle came true here on his final release. A great album, by far the best any Beatle has done in a while.

B00006LSM3|3.0|A fitting ending for a famously uneven solo career, Brainwashed is a nice improvement over the majority of George's releases following the majestic All Things Must Pass. Most of the tunes are acoustic guitar driven, with George's trademark electric slide woven throughout. True to his word, producer Jeff Lynne kept the production simple, thankfully without his ELO  signature sound that was so apparent on Cloud Nine. The guitar playing is well up in the mix, and George's playing at times is beyond what many probably thought his fretwork limitations were. Though George's voice sound a bit worn (which is understandable) it has aged well and fits the material to a tee. The lyrics are George wearing his thoughts and visions on his sleeve with a bit of venom here and a bit of humor there and a lot of resolution throughout. Pisces Fish, Looking for My Life and Stuck Inside a Cloud rank with some of his finest solo pieces. Marwa Blues is a stunning instrumental piece, laid back and peaceful. Overall, worth the wait. Sadly, our last wait for a George recording.

This should definitely be added to your Trio collection.

Interior scenes benefit from the detail as the richness of the Rivera hotel and restaurant locations can be savored. Come to think of it, this may be one of Mr. Hitchcock's most sensual movies, emphasizing the tactile: you see the patient fondling of the jewels, the enjoyment of the food (\\"..it's a quiche lorraine...\\")and wine and, by implication, the treat represented by the lovely Miss Kelly (\\"...have you ever had a better offer?...One that guarantees satisfaction?\\") It may well be, as many viwers have said, that this is a \\"lighter\\" Hitchcock. It is by no means less enjoyable. Grab it (and devour it with a quiche and a glass of crisp white wine. A muscadet). Sixth star, anyone?

0679735909|5.0|Possession is the story of two British literary scholars and their discovery of a century-old secret--the passionate relationship between two Victorian poets, the married and well-established Randolph Henry Ash, and the presumed lesbian feminist poet Christabel LaMotte.  The details of their brief affair are discovered slowly and inexorably through a trace of documents and linguistic ties: they are academic detectives.<br />Byatt prose is laced with literary allusions that any lover of British history and fiction will appreciate.  Notice for example that Maude Bailey is often described with reference to Yeat's muse because of her golden hair, or that Christabel LaMotte, whose family name suggests &quot;moth.&quot; she says &quot;no mere human can stand in a fire and not be consumed.&quot;  In every sense, the plot of this novel is dense, its characters tightly-knit.  Discovering hidden meanings within the names and places of this novel was half the fun of reading it.<br />Possession is also a montage of the remaining documents of its characters' lives, which LaMotte says is the end motive of all writers--the longing to be preserved on the dusty shelf.  The contemporary storyline is juxtaposed with letters between the lovers, the diaries of Ash's wife and LaMotte's lover, Blanche, and the poetry itself, which reveals clues about the relationship invisible to the typcial, merely academic reading.  Some of these documents drag, but then again they create a type of tension.  The reader feels much like the scholars must have felt, looking for subtleties that may reveal an entire narrative.  <br />Running parallel to the nineteenth century narrative are the personal lives of the academics Maude Bailey and Roland Michell, who stand to turn the world of Ash and La Motte criticism on their head with their discovery.  I actually enjoyed their story even more than that of the poets.  They seemed more real,  more intimate, more subtle.  And like their objects of academic desire,they as well battle with the questions of possession, of possession of a person, an idea, or the possession associated with the brief insanity that overcomes the lover.<br />This novel is very time and emotion intensive, which makes the somewhat abrupt ending rather surprising.  After carefully weaving a narrative that spans over a century, Byatt wraps up her novel with what can be compared to a chase scene.  However satisfying it is to have the clues of this mystery all tied up, it left me wondering about the author's motives at the end.  But perhaps that is precisely the point of the romance: abruptly ended, fleetingly passionate, like a moth to the flame.<br />Possession was made recently into a more than adequate film, though some of the essentials have been changed, especially with regard to Roland Michell, who is portrayed in the film as a very charasmatic and inquisitive American.

B00005JKNF|4.0|I actually managed not to hear about this movie at all before I saw it. The title sounded dimly familiar. I know it won awards, but so do most movies starring Tom Hanks, and I'm sorry, but if that's the official arbiter of excellence, I'll just stay home. Anyway, I read the back of the DVD and figured it would be one of those films that I'd find boring on first viewing, yet gradually begin to appreciate if I bothered with it any further. <br />Well, it IS rather \\"dry.\\" I found it odd that Altman claims that the \\"F-word\\" was deliberately insinuated into the script a sufficient number of times to secure an R rating - supposedly to keep teenage boys with too much testosterone from seeing \\"Gosford Park.\\" I should think the testosterone itself would do the job. Nothing explodes in this film, there's nary a lick of that crazy rock music the kids like, nobody gets killed...oh wait, somebody does get killed, but even the murder scene is impossibly dull and boring. Bloodless, antiseptic, British. <br />This is an \\"adult\\" film in the true sense. This is a film about adult social politics and intrigue and gossip, about the strange edgy relationship between these indefatiguably haughty upper-crust aristocrats and the ever-complacent servants they depend upon for every least personal function, yet whom they openly revile as congenitally inferior. It would be rash to presume that the plush and pampered \\"Upstairs\\" life is really any easier psychologically than the cramped and calloused \\"Downstairs\\" life, however. \\"Gosford Park\\" is an interesting investigation of what happens when the constraints and tabus of this sort of caste system render the lives of everyone involved practically intolerable. The servants have been just as thoroughly conditioned to automatic submission as outright slaves. Their entire existence is drudgery; their only dignity is vicarious; they acquire the pretensions and snobberies of their masters and often turn viciously and hypocritically on one another. Yet the aristocrats themselves, who never have to flex a muscle for the most trifling amenity, have been driven by inevitable boredom and inertia to likewise ruthlessly intrigue against each other purely to while away the time and maintain the status-quo. Their insanely regulated and mannered lifestyle is as nigh impossible for them to embody as it is for their servants to set up. As such, everybody all-around is miserable, conspiratory, two-faced, paranoid, vindictive, jealous, insecure, chasing after an ideal which can never be realized. This social climate is the perfect breeding ground for every sort of moral dis-ease. <br />Nice craft on everybody's part really nails this home. Each actor - from Maggie Smith as an excruciatingly pompous, impudent, willfully-helpless old mummy of a Lady, to Sir William McCordle as a fat, nasty, overbearing slob, to Kristen Scott Thomas as a harsh, withering, jaded, manipulative Marlene Dietrich doppelganger - was fitted with an individual mic so that multiple simultaneous conversations in a scene could be recorded and then mixed to allow salient bits to come through in the final cut. This invests \\"Gosford Park\\" with a certain realism and allows the viewer to feel like an invisible voyeur wandering amongst these complicated proceedings and catching both relevant and irrelevant snippets. <br />Am I recommending it? I suppose I am. I did enjoy this film precisely because I'm the type that tends to get caught up in the minutiae of period customs and conventions, which are evidently rendered with exacting accuracy in \\"Gosford.\\" The documentaries accompanying the DVD are very interesting and informative in this vein.

B0000640SA|5.0|Christopher Nolan is a genius. It isn't that he took an obscure little short story by his brother and used its gimmick of a man with memory loss to amuse us enough into staying with it to the end. What he's done here is take a gimmick & imbue it with deeper meaning than it was meant to have. In doing so, he's elevated a simple film noir to the level of existentialist classic and simultaneously reinvented the rules of film noir, a<BR>once dead genre. <br />    Nolan was smart enough to not resolve the old cliche of the amnesia victim like movies of yore where they just suddenly remember everything & then it's O.K. From the begining of the movie we get clued in that Leonard Shelby is going to be permanently scarred, and so he comes to represent ALL that is wrong with forgetting, including our own absent mindedness. <br />    It is this hook of the \\"unreliable narrator\\" that makes the movie endlessly fascinating. We begin to look for the one true cause of Leonard's problem. Is it his injury? Is it his notes? Is it people misleading him? Is he purposely misleading himself?<BR>In Nolan's film they are all dependent on one another, and the movie becomes a Rorshach blot for ourselves, represented by Leonard's constantly looking into mirrors & coming to absolute conclusions that shatter his world farther. He can never accept the tatoos built up over time in lieu of memory without being reminded of his wife's murder (Jorja Fox, later of CSI. Don't blink or you won't catch her). Even \\"Teddy\\"/Lt. Gamel clues him to missing pages only to be blotted out like an incorrect note.<br />    Plotwise, this movie stands in league with Kurosawa's Rashomon, its complement. In Rashomon, we get an individual questioning witnesses brought forth to tell stories to provide for clues. In Memento, we get clues provided for stories to tell witnesses brought forth to question an individual. Both movies point fingers at relative & absolute judgement being nothing without each other in the pursuitof justice.<br />    Nolan has reinvigorated film noir as a style for illustrating<BR>modern ills. Rather than using the old black & white shadows & tilted camera angles, however, he uses all the opposite tones, light, colors: the character being sure & naive rather than cynical & doubtful, the loud enamels of modern hotel rooms, instant polaroids instead of newsprint, characters who consider Leonard a nuisance instead of a threat, etc. This isn't completely origional on Nolan's part, Tavernier used it in Coup De Torchon, but Chris realizes its potential here.<br />   This has become one of my top five films ever. It still affects me.<br />    What you get with this Limited Edition is Nolan's commentary (sadly left out of the origional) & other behind the scenes stuff. My favorite part of it is the packaging & the convoluted entry screens that play on the theme of a psychiatric file filled with tests of perception that you have to take to play the movie. Not for those who want instant gratification or are easily frustrated, but lots of fun to those of us who like to stoke the paranoid atmosphere of the movie before watching.<br />   Christopher Nolan is the new post modern Hitchcock.<br />   A classic in every sense.

B00003CX9W|3.0|Having lived for years with the very distorted audio of the VHS version of FANTASIA, I tried the 2000 release of the 60th Anniversary Special Edition of FANTASIA (1940), with its \\"completely restored\\" label.  <br />The original FANTASIA was allegedly recorded with \\"perfect\\" fidelity using an 8 channel optical process developed for FANTASIA called \\"Fantasound\\".  At some point, the original sound track was apparently transferred over telephone lines.  In 1955 a magnetic recording was made to save the deteriorating optical copy.  The results were bad, and a new sound track was made in the 1980's, but it lacked the synergism of the Stokowski orignal, so they reverted to a best effort cleanup of the magnetic copy of the original.  In 2000 a \\"complete\\" restoration was made of what sounds like the magnetic copy.<br />Even though the 2000 \\"restoration\\" of the original score is better than the earlier VHS version, it is still distorted enough in places, that it is hard to stick with it. There is a lot of what I would call \\"modulation\\" of volume between different passages and much outright distortion at higher volumes. The voices are clearer, but still fuzzy in places.<br />Probably no one really knows what FANTASIA sounded like at its Premier in 1940, but this wouldn't hack it today.<br />The visual images have held up better.  Good color, artistic originality that hasn't grown too old, and incredibly detailed animation.  <br />I won't say anything about Disney's self censorship of rereleases of its own products, except that it's obvious where this movie was altered since something like a digital zoom was used.<br />This is probably the best soundtrack we will see for FANTASIA (1940) because it doesn't appear to be winning any viewing contests.  <br />This movie may be listed for Kids.  It probably should be \\"Family\\", and a moderately liberal family at that.  There are devils, witches, breasts and skeletons, naked human images and implications about evolution to go along with the dancing hippos and abstract musical images.<br />Bottom line: buy this one if you have to choose from what's available today.

B00006LSM3|5.0|God Bless George and keep him close to the heart of truth. The Quiet Beatle has said goodbye with an album that is every bit as remarkable as his first solo effort 30 years ago. The one least enchanted with being a Beatle and often relegated to secondary composer status, George eschewed all manner of ego centric efforts because he was ever and always focused on a truth more fundamental. Coupled with a brilliant sense of humour and an often brutal honesty, George always delivered the real deal. <br />This coda to a body of work that was always good and sometimes phenomenal deserves very high praise. Start to finish, the songs are terrific. Filled with his observations and sage admonitions, the man who wrote BEWARE OF DARKNESS opens up with ANY ROAD, a lighter take on the same theme. P2 is a brilliant Harrison ditty, like PIGGIES or CRACKERBOX PALACE. And then George sets about ripping your emotional guts out. LOOKING FOR MY LIFE, HIDDEN IN A CLOUD and the balance of the CD are as heart wrenching and honest an evaluation of a life as you'll ever hear anyone deliver. George is the anti-dote to Peter Gabriel. Great songs, exquisite guitar craft, and that voice that seemed to wobble at times and cajole and then offer some of the most incredibly beautiful love poems ever written are all in full display. His self deprecating gallows humour tackles BETWEEN THE DEVIL AND THE DEEP BLUE SEA, compleat with killer ukelele runs. At times he references musical ideas from his canon. I swear I can almost hear the Beatle and solo tunes to which he briefly refers in his playing and chord structures. MAGWA BLUES is a sublime response to WHILE MY GUITAR. His observations sneak up on you, they always did. He was ever and always understating his case, all the more for its impact to live with you long after.<br />Jeff Lynne and Dhani Harrison have rendered George's demo tapes beautifully. Lynne restrains his ELO excesses to an absolute minimum, and Dhani has brought tremendous love and sympathy to his father's work. The old man would be proud. Liner notes bid farewell to Paul and Ringo. Jim Keltner and Jools Holland contribute perfectly, and the ending of the disc is absolutely, categorically, beautifully George. We're going to miss him.

0151006903|4.0|I've been an unabashed fan of Eco's for nearly two decades now, and leapt into &quot;Baudolino&quot; enthusiastically, despite the sour taste that still lingers from &quot;Island of the Day Before.&quot;  Eco's gift is rare -- his erudition and his tangential details bring to vivid life not only far-away times and places, but also firmly root the reader in the psychological struggles of the protagonist.  &quot;The Name of the Rose,&quot; Eco's first and best novel, was a page-turning story and also made me feel the pressing weight of monastic politics.  &quot;Foucault's Pendulum,&quot; although set in the second half of the 20th century, convincingly sweeps the reader into a truly frightening dance with the occult.  All three readings of &quot;Foucault&quot; left me sleeping uneasily for several nights.<br />I thought of &quot;Island of the Day Before&quot; as Eco's first real stumble.  Neither the story nor the atmosphere seemed remotely as compelling as in either of the two previous novels.<br />In many respects, &quot;Baudolino&quot; is a return to form.  The return isn't complete, however.  Twelfth-century Europe springs to life here, although it doesn't ring true -- there seems to be an overly-modern sensibility written into the dialogue and into the motivations of most characters.  For example, the medieval archetype of unrequited courtly love is reduced to an episode reminiscent of the nubile Stepmom from &quot;Bill and Ted's Excellent Adventure.&quot;  <br />Perhaps &quot;Baudolino's&quot; greatest accomplishment is the cool eye it casts toward the very modern notion that a well-crafted lie exposes a greater truth.  Baudolino's proclamation to that effect seems much more benign than, say, the claims of Tawana Brawley -- but is it really?

B000066C8G|4.0|Zoolander is one of those films where a silly sense of humour is required.  It's based on the New York fashion scene, with dimwitted model Ben Stiller being brainwashed to assassinate the prime minister of Malaysia.  This seems essential to the big fashion corporations as the prime minister has passed new laws to end the child labour on which their careers survive.  By taking such a fantastic concept the film enters into a fairly expansive parody of fashion and people's preoccupations with it.<br />Although there are several scenes of note, it is Stiller's over-the-top, exaggerated performance (complete with silly walk and frown) that make this movie worth seeing. This is a parody but in no way a serious one and isn't afraid to have a little bit of harmless, stupid fun on the way.  When Stiller's character insists to his roommates that to alleviate their sadness they go to have orance mochas and they all break into dopy grins, it's one of the movie's best moments.<br />Obviously this is a subject that's easy to lampoon but you can't help but love a film that's as earnest as this.  Like Josie And The Pussycats made a point of saturated media imagery, so does Zoolander with a huge amount of celebrity cameos from Winona Ryder to Gwen Stefani to Vince Vaughn.  Even Stiller's parents are there.  Yet even though this movie belongs to Stiller, there's a nice supporting role from Christine Taylor (Stiller's wife and previously seen in The Wedding Singer and The Craft).  Ultimately this is a movie that will make you feel good, but not in a sappy way, and thankfully even the happy conclusion has a fair bit of humour injected into it.  This isn't as good as the sharply observed Meet The Parents, but it's still one of Stiller's best.

B000001FE0|4.0|Reviewers have observed that this album is the Bee Gees' &quot;White Album.&quot; I beg to differ: it's really their &quot;Smiley Smile.&quot; The Odessa concept died in direct relation to the band's personal problems, in similar fashion to the Beach Boys and their legendary &quot;Smile&quot; album (the remnants of which were hastily thrown together as &quot;Smiley Smile&quot; after Brian Wilson could continue no further with it). At about the midpoint of the production of Odessa, things really started to go badly for the Bee Gees. Robin, looking for a way to have some creative control, left after it was decided Barry's &quot;First of May&quot; would make a better single than his &quot;Lamplight.&quot; That vital third seems, from listening, to be somewhat unenthusiastic about some of the tracks on this album.<br />The boys started recording with a grand design: a concept album made up of letters and observations written by an English sailor shipwrecked, stranded and alone aboard an unapproachable iceberg, doomed to wander the icy Black Sea for an indeterminate amount of time. The general feel of the album, I would speculate, is intact in the title track. A few compositions, like &quot;First of May,&quot; &quot;Seven Seas Symphony&quot; and &quot;You'll Never See My Face Again&quot; share the general atmosphere of that track and the lyrics fit thematically. But, as a result of discord within the band, the album was never finished as intended. Instead, current material seems to have been added to fill it out and it was released with its full potential unrealized.<br />If the band could have maintained the overall theme and mood as established in the core Odessa tracks, it could have been a near masterpiece. But the band was moving musically in a direction away from orchestral pop, a style they had developed via such seminal tracks as &quot;World&quot; and &quot;Cucumber Castle,&quot; from previous records, and mastered by the time of Odessa, embracing instead styleswhich were coming into fashion just then: country and folk rock. &quot;Marley Purt Drive&quot; provides the tell-tale link to such future soft-country albums as Life in a Tin Can. This didn't bode well: when the ground-breaking Main Course came out, with the blueprint of disco coded within, it was hoped this newest change in direction would light a fire under its flagging fan base (it did).<br />In any event... the true potential of the group lay on the control room floor in bits and pieces after the Odessa sessions.  Never again would an orchestra and a pop group meld in such a perfect way as on the remnant Odessa tracks (sorry Moody Blues fans).
B00006YXEK|4.0|There is no 'buyer beware' here.  Often with posthumous releases, you never know how 'ready' the artist had his work.  'Brainwashed' from the legendary George Harrison has everything but a George here on earth to promote it and possibly tour with it.  It's great, not 'All Things Must Pass' great, but great compared to much of what passes for music in 2002.  If you liked The Traveling Wilburys, you'll love it.  If you liked 'Cloud Nine' you'll love it -- in many ways it's 'Cloud' Part 2.  And if you loved him as a Beatle, there's no way you won't like it.  Lots of juicy slide guitar.  It's also very topical. Jeff Lyne, of ELO fame/Wilburys, lends the production hand and, fortunately, doesn't make it into an ELO album.  It's 'solo George' pure and simple, and there will never be anything like it again.  As the sticker bluntly says: The Final Studio Album.  Thanks for leaving it behind, George.  A tip: spend the extra few bucks and get the enhanced/with DVD version rather than just the audio CD, absolutely beautifully packaged and makes a better 'gift' to give.

B00006LSM3|4.0|Even if you didn't take into consideration the traumatic and painful events of George Harrison's final years, one would hail Brainwashed as a masterful album, one of the best \\"solo Beatle\\" efforts ranking up there with George's own All Things Must Pass and Cloud Nine, Starr's Ringo, McCartney's Band On the Run, and Lennon's Imagine. <br />From the opening statement of \\"give me plenty of that guitar\\" on the Wilbury-esque \\"Any Road\\" to the final Hindu chant at the end of the title track and album, Brainwashed is an upbeat, introspective, and supremely poignant record. Expertly polished by longtime Harrison collaborator Jeff Lynne, and son Dhani, it is the clearest glimpse into the heart and soul of a reluctant rock legend.<br />The overall sound of the album is similar to Harrison's previous efforts with Lynne...the Traveling Wilburys, the new tracks from Best of Dark Horse, and Cloud Nine. And while there are no obvious singles that might crack the Top Ten, keep in mind that there weren't any hits from Sgt. Pepper's either.<br />Highlights include \\"Any Road\\", the acerbic \\"Vatican Blues\\" and \\"Brainwashed\\", and the lovely \\"Never Get Over You\\" and \\"Stuck Inside a Cloud\\". Though \\"Pisces Fish\\" is somewhat tedious, the album quickly regains its stride with the best original song on the album, \\"Rising Sun\\". Featuring strings, slide guitar and minor chords it's Harrison in top form, with a latter-day Beatles/All Things Must Pass aura. \\"Between the Devil and the Deep Blue Sea\\" has George playing the ukelele with Jools Holland on piano and Joe Brown on acoustic guitar. It might be the simplest, happiest moment of George's solo career, and brings the album to it's apex. As Dhani Harrison said, \\"it reeks of my dad\\", and this is certainly the image George wanted to leave us with...a regular guy, having fun fooling around with an old standard, an old ukelele, and some old friends. A fitting coda, indeed.

B00003CXDD|3.0|Like most George Lucas projects, WILLOW has a lot of elements in it that feel very familiar. As with the STAR WARS movies (drawn from the FLASH GORDON serials, Jack Kirby's \\"New Gods\\" comics, the King Arthur legends, Doc Smith's LENSMAN novels, Kurosawa's samurai films, the WWII movie THE DAMBUSTERS, Edgar Rice Burroughs' \\"Barsoom\\" books, and so forth) WILLOW \\"pays homage\\" to a herd of sources, such as Tolkien, the Moses and/or Jesus birth legends, Tolkien, Walt Disney's SNOW WHITE, PINOCCHIO, and PETER PAN adaptations, Tolkien, Swift's GULLIVER'S TRAVELS, Tolkien, L. Frank Baum's \\"Oz\\" books, Tolkien, and his own initial STAR WARS trilogy. Oh, and Tolkien. Unlike the first couple of STAR WARS movies, WILLOW fails to grow into anything more organic than the sum of its many parts, remaining to the end a handsomely mounted mishmash of fantasy cliches, directed with reasonable efficiency by former AMERICAN GRAFFITI star and HAPPY DAYS survivor Ron Howard.<BR> So, why three stars? It's not easy to explain, even to myself. Frankly, I like the movie better than it deserves to be liked. Warwick Davis is appealingly gentle as Willow, and Jean Marsh has fun as Bavmorda, a.k.a. the Wicked Queen from Disney's SNOW WHITE, curling her lip and cackling hatefully. The two brownies, Lenny and Squiggy, are pretty funny, and Billy Barty has the best line in the movie (\\"Ignore the bird; follow the river!\\"). Some of the FX are impressive, not surprisingly, as they're the only things Lucas has really put any effort into since the early eighties. There's an enjoyable wizard's duel at the climax, during which, in a great down-to-earth moment, the aged sorceress Fin Raziel abandons magic and resorts to punching Bavmorda in the face repeatedly; there's also a two-headed dragon that's so stupid it manages to blow one of its own heads off. Occasionally the movie is unintentionally funny. The beautiful Joanne Whalley, cast as Bavmorda's supposedly menacing soldier-daugter Sorsha, wears a suit of black armor that seems to be three of four sizes too big for her; she looks exactly like Darth Vader's cute little daughter playing dress-up in Daddy's clothes. There's further costume humor later when Val Kilmer, as Han Solo wannabe Madmartigan, briefly dons a plumed helmet that resembles a huge Christmas ornament. Kilmer also does a drag routine that manages to get a laugh precisely because it's so embarrasingly corny; the fact that the filmmakers thought that it was funny turns out to be funny in itself. And then there are all those pigs. In fact, WILLOW is significantly more entertaining than, say, petrified duds like the two (so far) STAR WARS prequels, not to mention Ron Howard's holiday abomination HOW THE GRINCH STOLE CHRISTMAS. When WILLOW was initially released, the pretentious Lucasfilm hype that accompanied it was enough to skew one's judgement; George, we were pompously told, had \\"studied myths from around the world before defining WILLOW to his satisfaction.\\" Yeah, right. And Tolkien. He also claimed that it was \\"a pure fantasy that came right out of my psyche\\". As Pauline Kael wrote, \\"Maybe only a movie mogul can believe that he's the source of the world's treasury of legends and movies\\". Now that some time has passed, and having seen Lucas virtually lose touch with the very concepts of storytelling and drama, WILLOW looks pretty watchable now; a first-rate guilty pleasure, if nothing else.

B00006YXEK|5.0|One year after his death, George Harrison's final recording sessions are issued.  Frankly, I was expecting a somewhat patchwork album with seams showing, given George's musical inactivity in recent years, his lack of attention to current pop music trends, and his serious health problems.  Yet after hearing BRAINWASHED I'm convinced that George left us with his strongest, most consistent solo work since ALL THINGS MUST PASS.  What immediately grabs the listener is that George sounds rejuvenated and energetic throughout the album--not only his singing, but also his instrumental work.  Furthermore, his songwriting is recharged, offering tunes with attractive hooks and lyrics reflective of George's spiritual vision.  If anything his songwriting is positively rather than negatively affected by the long layoff...while some tracks stand out more than others, there are no weak spots on BRAINWASHED.  <br />Jeff Lynne and George's son Dhani co-produced the album: George's keep-it-basic musical approach is maintained throughout, with little in the way of outside influences or contemporary musical strains (fortunately the results sound timeless, not dated).  The tablas that enter on the closing title track add some variables to the mix...probably the one thing I would have liked to hear more of would be for a bit more usage of such outside elements, but that is a minor point.  The bottom line is that BRAINWASHED is an important work not only from a post-Beatles perspective, but its musical integrity and spiritual vibe may make a considerable impression on the contemporary music scene that George admittedly had little affinity for.       <br />P.S., Although only 7 minutes in length, the bonus DVD will be of considerable interest to even casual fans.  It is a mini-documentary of the BRAINWASHED sessions, featuring footage and interviews with George, Dhani, and Lynne.  Although in the midst of the health problems that took his life, George seems happy and in a musically-productive state-of-mind.  My only regret with this DVD is that the documentary wasn't longer, with more extensive musical footage.

It is natural that such a business assumes elephantine proportions in terms of size and product portfolio. Soon it is accused of monopolistic practices and the elephant slows down. In business terms it turns into a white elephant, slow and sick, on the verge of being dismantled; everyone looking for their fair share of the ivory. Such an elephant needs special treatment, and the ideal person to put it back on its feet, and bring in the agility of a horse should be adept at the skills of a mahout and the training of a Vet. IBM had a dress code that again had outlived its times. I remember a joke during the early days of IBM in India: \\" If you see a man dressed in a three piece suit crossing the road on a hot summer noon in Delhi, chances are that he is mad or he is from IBM.\\" <br />IBM's basic Beliefs were misinterpreted and practiced contrary to the very purpose of their origins. \\"Superior customer service\\" came to mean, \\"Servicing our machines on customers' premises,\\" instead of paying real attention to their business. \\" Excellence in everything we do\\" became an obsession with perfection and a led to several layers of checks and approvals, slowing down decision making and delaying product launches and response times. \\" Respect for the individual\\" soon became an interpretation for life long employment, entitlement for rich benefits and the freedom to ignore organizational priorities over that of the individuals'.As a group, the employees served themselves better than they did their customers. <br />Among the various issues on product portfolio, pricing and customer service, the greatest challenge faced by Lou appears to be on the cultural front at IBM.
